大雨倾盆，库尔特被迫接受这样一个事实：尽管他做了许多准备，但今晚的比赛并不顺利。今天早上，他一直担心自己会吓到莫里茨，担心自己会失去这个可靠、安全、自信的男人的尊重。他在冰冷的公寓里踱来踱去，直到脚踝关节咔哒作响和痛苦。但他没有为这个结果做好充分准备。如果他准备得更充分，他可能就不会犯下这个严重的错误：他给莫里茨提供了安慰，但这种安慰被一种令他如此放心、如此坚定、如此真实的人所厌恶的精神主义所包裹。他本应该停下来，但他停不下来，所以他走得太远，暴露了他无法证明的东西、没有人会接受的东西、会让他发疯的东西、最好自己保留的东西。

Pelted by fat juicy drops of rain, Kurt is forced to accept that for all his preparations, tonight did not go well. He worried endlessly this morning that he might spook Moritz, that he might lose the respect of this solid, safe, sure man. He paced the floor of his frozen apartment until his ankle joints clicked and ached. But he had not properly prepared for this outcome. If he had been better prepared, he might not have made this grave mistake: He offered Moritz consolation, wrapped in a spiritualism that is repugnant to the man he finds so reassuring, so solid, so real. He should have stopped, but he couldn’t stop, and so he went too far and exposed what he can’t prove, what no one will ever accept, what will drive him mad, what is better off kept to himself.

他整个身子瘦弱地恳求道：“莫里茨”，俯身看着大理石桌面和桌面上的液体线条。他的话被拉长成了哀叹：“我们无法证明它是真的，但我们可以认识到它是真的。我们的头脑可以看到真理。即使数学无法看到真理，我们也能看得见。我们的头脑比数学更宽广。我们的头脑更宽广。

His whole thin strand of a body pleaded, “Moritz,” leaning in over the marble table and the liquid lines on its surface. His words were elongated into a lament, “We cannot prove it is true, but we can recognize that it is true. Our minds can see truth. See it even when mathematics cannot. Our minds are bigger than that. Our minds are bigger.

“我同意你的观点：除了逻辑和数字，我们什么都不能相信，什么都不能相信。我们不知道什么是真实的。我不知道你是否存在。你也不知道我是否存在。你也许相信这是真的。但你永远不会知道，莫里茨。永远不会。我们唯一能确定的是 1+1=2。我们的思想可以触及它。

“I agree with you in this much: we can trust nothing, believe nothing, except logic and numbers. We don’t know what's real. I do not know if you exist. You do not know if I exist. You may believe it to be true. But you will never know, Moritz. Never. All that we can ever be sure of is that 1+1=2. Our minds can touch it.

“数字就像太阳和地球一样真实。它们占据着一个与物理现实不同的现实，但并不因此而变得不真实。心灵是通往这个世界的大门，通往另一个现实的大门，一个完美的地方，其他一切都消失了。莫里茨，世界并非如此。”

“Numbers are as real as the sun and the earth. They occupy a reality separate from this physical reality, but no less real for that. The mind is a gateway to this world, a gateway to another reality, a perfect place where everything else falls away. The world, Moritz, is not all that is the case.”

作为回应，他那些围坐在小桌子旁的朋友、同事、伙伴，这些陌生人，都非常安静地继续抽烟、在酒吧点酒、整理私人想法。奥托从胡须上掸掉蛋糕屑一边把糖霜往嘴里送。桌子上很安静，库尔特可以听到奥托的舌头湿润地咀嚼上颚的声音。当所有可见的糖分都分解后，奥托用咖啡冲走了粘在牙龈上的凝胶。其他人喝完最后一口酒，然后默默地盯着杯肚，仿佛想从湿漉漉的糖粉中预测他们的命运。莫里茨的目光从桌子上扫过，没有特别看向任何人；他的双臂仍然交叉着。沉默了很长一段时间后，莫里茨用力吸了一口气，抿紧嘴唇，双手交叉放在桌边，好像是第一次参加会议。然后，他打开了一封来自哥廷根同事的信，巧妙而果断地将谈话引向了另一个方向，其他人似乎很感激地跟进了。

In response, his friends who were collected around the small table, his colleagues, his associates, these strangers, very quietly continued to smoke and order from the bar and sort through private thoughts. Otto brushed cake crumbs from his whiskers while simultaneously pressing frosting into his mouth. The table was quiet enough that Kurt could hear the moist mashing of Otto's tongue against his upper palate. When every visible trace of sugar had disintegrated, Otto washed the gel sticking to his gums down his throat with a bath of coffee. The others sucked down the last of their drinks and then stared mute at the belly of their cups as if to divine their fortunes from the wet grinds. Moritz's gaze glanced off the table and struck no one in particular; his arms were still crossed. After a distinctly extended silence, Moritz inhaled strongly, pressed his lips together, and rested his interlaced hands on the edge of the table as if joining the proceedings for the first time. Then, unfolding a letter from a colleague in Göttingen, he carried the conversation delicately yet decisively in another direction, and the others seemed grateful to follow.

科特因无法完全理解的羞愧感而哽咽，他从椅子上跳了起来，给奥托留下了深刻的印象，然后他推开奥尔加，冲过房间，然后从咖啡馆摔到了街上。

Choking on a shame he didn’t fully understand, Kurt burst out of his chair, impressing Otto until he jostled Olga and barged through the room before fairly falling out of the café and onto the street.

他跑进下一个区，向左转，擦过一栋建筑的圆形后墙，然后又被抛向前方，穿过另一条大街。这些潮湿的街道上空无一人，只有楼上公寓明亮的窗户映出人影。科特用脚把水溅开。水花溅落到无处，等水花平息后，水花又出现在他身后。

He runs into the next district, turning left and brushing against the round back of a building until he is slung forward again, across another Strasse. These damp streets are empty except for the reflections in the puddles of people in bright windows in the flats above him. Kurt's foot splashes them away. They slop off to nothing only to reappear behind him when the ripples have settled.

一个小女孩从窗外望出去，想知道谁在地上，我们是怎么到这里来的，为什么。她欣赏着树梢和一个孤独、笨拙的男人在奔跑，她想想其他人也发现了自己的问题：我们并不了不起。我们困惑、聪明、愚笨，是迷失的活着的灰烬。

A young girl looks out of a window and wonders who is down on the ground and how we got here and why. She admires the tops of the trees and a lone, clumsy man running and she thinks what so many others have discovered of themselves: We are not much. We are confused and brilliant and stupid, lost clumps of living ash.

我试图置身于这些故事之外，但我也在这里。我站在城市的街道上。在去火车的路上。我看到窗户上的女孩。她看到我在这里。她是我头脑中的一个薄弱的虚构。或者我是她的虚构。或者你的虚构。

I have tried to stay out of these stories but I am out here too. I am standing on a street in a city. On my way to a train. I see the girl up there in the window. She sees me out here. She is a thin invention of my own mind. Or I am an invention of hers. Or of yours.

哥德尔也看见了她，窗外的那个女孩。然后她走了。窗外空无一物。她去办一件她已经办过一百次的差事，或者去洗那些需要反复清洗的盘子，或者去写一封永远无法表达她所有期望的信。

Gödel sees her too, the girl in the window. Then she's gone. The window is empty. She has gone to run an errand she has run a hundred times or to wash dishes that will need to be washed again and again or to start a letter that will never express everything she hopes from it.

哥德尔沿着光滑的黄铜轨道疾驰而过，一路上轻微打滑，经过参差不齐的树木和脏兮兮的路沿，穿过另外两个区域，终于找到了这个地方，敲了一下旧招牌。一个留着浓密灰胡子的无聊男子探进半开的门缝，说：“演出结束了。”

Gödel charges across the tram tracks, slick brass veins, skidding slightly, past periodically staggered trees and soiled curbs, through two more districts until he finds the place and bangs on the old sign. A bored man bearing a dense gray mustache leans into the gap made by the fractionally open door to say, “Show's over.”

“阿黛尔。”科特喘着气说，“阿黛尔。”

“Adele.” Kurt wheezes, “Adele.”

纽约市

NEW YORK CITY

我试图置身于这些故事之外，但我也在这里。我站在城市的街道上。在去乘火车的路上。我可以看到一个人站在一栋俯瞰公园的华丽建筑的几层楼高的窗户里。如果我在那栋楼里，我也会向窗外望去，想知道谁在下面，我们是如何到这里来的，为什么，我会承认我已经承认过一千遍的事情：我们并不重要。我们困惑、聪明、愚蠢，是迷失的活灰烬。

I have tried to stay out of these stories but I am out here too. I am standing on a street in a city. On my way to a train. I can see someone standing in a window several stories high in an ornate building overlooking the park. If I were up in that building, I’d look out of the window too and wonder who was down on the ground and how we got here and why and I would admit what I have already admitted a thousand times: We are not much. We are confused and brilliant and stupid, lost clumps of living ash.

此时此刻，这条街上有一位穿着大号旧外套的老妇人。她沿着大街走着，我轻而易举地追上了她。在老式路灯和公园的映衬下，仿佛又过了十年。如果她真的老了，我会想起奥尔加，但奥尔加在荷兰因肾手术去世，享年 55 岁。

On this street at this hour is an old woman in a huge old coat. I overtake her easily as she makes her way down the avenue. With the old fashioned streetlights and the backdrop of the park, it could be another decade altogether. She reminds me of Olga if she had grown so old, but Olga died in the Netherlands at the age of fifty-five after a kidney operation.

一位女士和她的狗朝我走来，从我两边经过。她放下狗的皮带，这样我就可以轻松地踩上去一转眼，我的脚悬空了，一瞬间，我变成了一个准备跳绳的小女孩，她变成了我的朋友，正在挥动跳绳的把手。然后她和那只狗都消失了，连同那种感觉。

A woman and her dog walk toward me and move to pass on either side. She lowers the dog's leash so that I can easily step over and for a fraction of a second while my foot is suspended in the air, I’m a little girl about to skip rope and she's my friend swinging the jump rope handle. Then she and the dog are gone, along with the sensation.

公园里，矮墙那边，两个女孩在草地上玩耍。巨人俯视着她们的玩具，比例失调。她们手拉手旋转，身体向后倾斜，直到她们的手指紧紧相扣，胖乎乎的肉和骨头缠在一起。我看到了什么？她们中心周围的角动量。她们运动中的物理原理。一个女孩对沾满青草的膝盖的记忆。

In the park, over the low wall, there are two girls playing in the grass. Giants looming over their toys, monstrously out of proportion. They’re holding hands and spinning, leaning farther and farther back until their fingers rope together, chubby flesh and bone enmeshed. What do I see? Angular momentum around their center. A principle of physics in their motion. A girlish memory of grass-stained knees.

我继续往前走，远离女孩们对世界机制的自信。我相信它们是存在的，即使我对它们的了解并不完美，只是对它们数十亿个振动原子的粗略描绘。我相信这是真的。这些女孩不是我想象的产物，我也不是你的产物。有些事情我们无法严格证明。所以让我们把它当成是真的吧，这就是我。

I keep walking and recede from the girls’ easy confidence in the world's mechanisms. I believe they exist, even if my knowledge of them can only be imperfect, a crude sketch of their billions of vibrating atoms. I believe this to be true. The girls are not a product of my imagination and I am not a product of yours. There are just some things that we can’t strictly prove. So let's take it to be true, this is me.

我在宇宙中穿梭，途中遇见了一些女孩、一个少年、一只狗和一个老妇人。也许我应该列出更多塑造我与真相关系的重要事件，但这个故事也是谎言。我本可以完全以不同的方式写这本书，但话又说回来，也许这本书是唯一可能的方式，而这些也是我唯一能做的选择。这就是我，一个不真实的复合体，也许是个骗子，也许不自由。

I am on an orbit through the universe that crosses the paths of some girls, a teenager, a dog, an old woman. Maybe I should list more significant events that shaped my relationship to truth but that story would be a lie too. I could have written this book entirely differently, but then again maybe this book is the only way it could be, and these are the only choices I could have made. This is me, an unreal composite, maybe part liar, maybe not free.

英国剑桥。1935 年

CAMBRIDGE, ENGLAND. 1935

艾伦·图灵被活埋近一小时，七年后，他躺在格兰切斯特草地的草地上。他偶然来到这里。他离开国王学院的房间，到剑桥市中心的露天市场寻找苹果。第一个摊位上的红苹果皮通常是他最喜欢的，上面贴着干枯的生菜残渣，这是清晨交通事故的证据。在最初的厌恶之后，他考虑买下这些水果，然后洗干净，但这个选择被附近的红甜菜打乱了。这些作物的颜色并不完全相同，但它们属于同一科。一些甜菜碰到了一些苹果，而苹果又碰到了其他苹果，形成了一连串溢出的深红色污染了箱子，破坏了他的胃口。他考虑过买一个黄色或绿色的样本，直到他发现一个年轻人几乎没有其他农产品，除了一箱放在桶上的红苹果。

Seven years after the day that he is buried alive for nearly a full hour, Alan Turing lies in the grass of Grantchester Meadows. He came here by accident. He left his rooms in King's College in search of an apple in the open-air market in Cambridge's town center. The red apple skins in the first stall, usually his favorite, were papered with the withered remains of lettuce, evidence of an early morning transportation accident. After his initial revulsion, he considered buying the fruit and washing it clean but that option was dashed by the proximity of the red beets. The colors of the crops weren’t exactly the same but they were in the same family. And some of the beets were touching some of the apples, which in turn touched other apples, forming a chain of spilled crimson contaminating the box and spoiling his appetite. He considered settling for a yellow or green specimen until he spotted a young bloke displaying scarcely any other produce except a crate of red apples balanced on a barrel.

他温柔地对待庄稼和男孩，但只带走了果实。

He made a gentle approach to both the harvest and the boy but walked away with only the fruit.

他的男朋友（前男友）詹姆斯离开剑桥已经快六个月了，艾伦的孤独感让他感到一种熟悉的忧郁。他把红苹果放在蓝色夹克的口袋里，撩起衣领，系好纽扣，走在小银街的旧鹅卵石上，穿过牛栏，沿着米尔池走到羊草地，远离了散发着不同发酵阶段啤酒浓烈气味的破败米尔酒吧。不久之后，他便不由自主地开始了前往格兰切斯特草地的愉快徒步旅行。

It has been nearly six months since his boyfriend (former boyfriend) James left Cambridge, and Alan's loneliness has fostered a familiar melancholy. With his red apple in the pocket of his blue jacket, he lifted his collar, fastened his buttons, and found himself walking atop the old cobblestones of little Silver Street through the cattle gate onto Sheep's Green along Mill Pond and away from the slumping Mill Pub with its strong smell of beer in various stages of fermentation. Before long he was involuntarily committed to the pleasant hike to Grantchester Meadows.

他躺在最深处的绿色牧场里，剑桥郡上方特殊的湿气使牧场变得凉爽。现在是下午早些时候，但仅从光线判断，可能已经是晚上了。如果他转过头，让散射的光线聚集在坚硬的蜡质草叶上，大片凹凸不平的草地看起来几乎是蓝色的。在他上方高处，烧焦的碳被风吹动，就像香烟灰一样，直到灰烬变成一群小黑鸟。它们齐声成群。虽然有条不紊，但没有旋律——身份不明的黑色鸟儿无声无息地飞翔，然后它们就飞走了。

He lies in the deepest of green pastures, quenched as they are by the special damp trapped atop Cambridgeshire. It is the early afternoon, although judging by light alone it might as well be evening. If he turns his head so the diffuse light collects on the stiff blades of waxy grass, big lumpy patches of the meadow look almost blue. High above him charred carbon is driven by the wind, like ash flicked from a cigarette until the ash resolves into a flock of little black birds. They swarm out of unison. Orchestrated but not melodic—the silent atonal drifts of unidentified black fowl and then they’re gone.

艾伦目光呆滞地看着这些鸟，他的眼前浮现出一个幽灵印记——白色的鸟儿渐渐黯淡无光。他眼中浮现出尘土的影像。临时棺材里浮现出海盐碎片的幻影。

Alan watches the birds flatly; a ghost imprint speckles his vision—white birds paling to nothing. An image of dust in his eyes. A phantom of shards of sea salt in a makeshift coffin.

为了抵抗沮丧，他经常跑到这里或沿着河边跑，直到他觉得如果他不在离学院十英里的地方折返，他可能会一直跑到伦敦。有时他会留在如果他有正在进行的实验或要参加研讨会，他就会靠近他的房间。然后，他会在剑河最窄的地方安顿下来，浑浊的河水中有一卷泥浆，融入草丛中，河水几乎要干涸了，然后才分流，仍然在视线之内，形成了一个适当的水流，一片大到足以让平底船和划船者漂浮的水体。无论他注定要占据哪个位置，他都会被吸引到外面潮湿的地方，在云层之下。

Resisting despondency, he often runs here or along the river until it seems he might run all the way to London if he doesn’t turn back within ten miles of the college. Sometimes he stays close to his rooms, if he has an experiment effervescing or a seminar to attend. And then he settles at the narrowest part of the river Cam, a roll of mud out of the opaque water merging into grass, a mere nozzle where the river nearly dies before it diverges, still within sight, to a proper flow, a body of water big enough to float the punts and rowers. Regardless of which spot he's destined to occupy, he is drawn outside into the damp and below the accumulation of clouds.

但草地是他最喜欢的地方。他伸开四肢，深深地陷在草丛和泥土中，想象自己是泥土和植物，一袋无生命的分子重组成一个人。从大多数角度看，他都隐身在草丛中，几乎可以从他最喜欢的地面凹陷处看到整个半球形的天空。不止一次，他让一个徒步旅行者惊恐不已，那个徒步旅行者大步走过他躺在地上，看着地层，他有一次被自行车撞倒了。

But the meadow is his favorite spot. He stretches out, deep in the grass and dirt, and imagines he is mud and vegetation, a bag of inanimate molecules reordered into a man. He is invisible in the grass from most angles and can almost see a full half sphere of sky from his favorite recess in the ground. More than once he has traumatized a hiker who strode upon him lying there looking into the stratum, and once he was struck by a bicycle.

在这些最孤独的日子里，他能感觉到克里斯的存在。他大学宿舍的窗台上放着一张他珍爱的克里斯照片——一张模模糊糊的照片，模糊的反面印刷。这张照片激发了艾伦的幻想，比一根刺或走过热煤更能引起他的哀悼。收到照片后，他给克里斯的母亲写了一张纸条：

He senses Chris's presence on these loneliest days. Propped on the windowsill of his college rooms is a cherished photo of Chris—a poor likeness, the unfocused image printed in reverse. The picture helps encourage Alan's fantasies and elicits his mourning more effectively than a thorn in his side, or a walk over hot coals. On receipt of the photo he writes a note to Chris's mother:

亲爱的莫尔科姆夫人，

我很高兴收到克里斯的照片。它提醒我们克里斯现在在某种程度上还活着。人们可能过于倾向于只考虑他未来还活着我们什么时候才能再次见到他呢？但如果认为他只是暂时与我们分离，那就更有帮助了。

My dear Mrs. Morcom,

I was so pleased to receive the photo of Chris. It reminds us that Chris is in some ‘way alive now. One is perhaps too inclined to think only of him alive at some future time when we shall meet him again; but it is really so much more helpful to think of him as just separated from us for the present.

艾伦有道德义务去实现克里斯无法实现的目标，因为从某种意义上说，克里斯现在还活着。一个人不会因为脑部物质的崩溃而解体。精神是永恒的本质。灵魂的力量恰恰在于它没有与过去或未来紧密相连，而是悬而未决地存在，渗透到时间中。

Alan has a moral obligation to achieve what Chris no longer can, because in some sense Chris is alive now. An individual doesn’t just disintegrate with the breakdown of his brain matter. The spirit is a timeless essence. The soul's strength is precisely that it is not properly stitched to the past or to the future, but rather hangs there out of sequence and permeates time.

于是艾伦感受到了克里斯的挑战。在克里斯不在的时候，他试图模仿克里斯，这也让他感觉没那么可笑。艾伦的模仿是一种粗糙、误导性的复制，因此不会被任何人发现。这个负担比克里斯在世时要轻一些，克里斯·莫尔科姆的不懈成功与艾伦·图灵的平淡失败形成了鲜明的对比。虽然克里斯没有活着获得奖学金，但他获得了最珍贵的奖学金——剑桥大学三一学院的入学资格。在渴望靠近克里斯的驱使下，以及在克里斯的榜样的驱使下，艾伦努力振作起来。尽管屡屡失败，艾伦最终还是​​获得了剑桥大学国王学院的奖学金，这是他仅次于三一学院的选择。

And so Alan feels Chris's challenge. He also feels somewhat less ridiculous attempting the imitation in his absence. There is no danger that anyone will detect the emulation as Alan's is a crude, misguided reproduction. This burden is somewhat lighter than when Chris was alive, and Chris Morcom's relentless successes contrasted harshly against Alan Turing's tepid failures. Although he did not live to take up the fellowship, Chris had earned the most prized of all scholarships, a place at Trinity College in Cambridge. Driven by his longing to be near Chris, and by his example, Alan struggled to drag himself up. Despite persistent failures, Alan finally obtained a fellowship to King's College in Cambridge, his choice second only to Trinity.

从那以后，他就一直住在这里。几乎受到了欢迎。即使不是完全的圈内人，至少也不是那么被排斥。甚至他的性取向也在没有受到直接迫害的情况下发展。他有一两个不错的朋友。但他仍然想念克里斯，甚至偶尔和莫尔科姆夫人一起度假，而不是和自己的母亲一起去吉尔福德。

And here he has been ever since. Almost welcome. If not a complete insider, then at least not such an outcast. Even his sexuality has evolved without outright persecution. And he has a decent friend or two. But still he misses Chris and even spends occasional holidays with Mrs. Morcom instead of in Guildford with his own mother.

克里斯去世后，艾伦开始与克里斯夫人进行生硬的交流。莫尔康姆夫人试图释放一些悲伤。不久之后，这些僵硬而礼貌的信件就因情绪而变得僵硬。他几乎承认了自己的爱，使用了“吸引”这个词，比她可能喜欢的要多，但暗示要么被忽略，要么被升华。莫尔康姆夫人在自己的悲伤中绝望，寻找任何悲伤的同伴，即使是这个她几乎不记得儿子提到过的陌生男孩。

After Chris's death, Alan began a stilted exchange with Mrs. Morcom to release some of his grief. It wasn’t long before the stiff, polite letters buckled with emotion. He as much as confessed his love, using the word “attracted” more than she might have liked, but the implications were either ignored or sublimated. Mrs. Morcom was desperate in her own grief and sought any companions in sorrow, even this strange boy whom she could scarcely remember her son mentioning.

艾伦认为他对死者的依恋是正当的，因为从某种意义上来说克里斯仍然存在。为了帮助他为自己辩护，他给莫尔科姆夫人写了一篇题为“灵魂的本质”的文章，他在文章中写道：

Alan justifies his attachment to the deceased since in a sense Chris still exists. To help formulate his defense he drafted an essay to Mrs. Morcom entitled “Nature of Spirit” in which he wrote:

科学界曾经认为，如果在某一特定时刻，宇宙的一切都是已知的，那么我们就可以预测“未来它会是什么样子”。但是，随着我们对原子的研究，这一理论可能会失效。我们有一种意志，它能够决定原子的活动，可能在大脑的一小部分，也可能在大脑的整个部分……当身体死亡时，身体的“机制”——支撑灵魂的机制——就消失了，灵魂迟早会找到一个新的身体，也许是立即找到。

It used to be supposed in Science that if everything was known about the Universe at any particular moment then we can predict ‘what it will be through all the future… . But this theory may break down as we study atoms. We have a will ‘which is able to determine the action of the atoms probably in a small portion of the brain, or possibly all over it… . When the body dies the “mechanism” of the body, holding the spirit, is gone and the spirit finds a new body sooner or later, perhaps immediately.

尽管艾伦对自己的信仰感到不安——这种信仰从未像他希望的那样具体化——但他并没有让精神上的宽容破坏他对数学的纯粹看法。为了向莫尔科姆致敬，图灵以明确的数学细节分析了二氧化硫和碘酸。在微分方程和化学成分之下，他发现了一个清晰的结果。清晰而真实。他用黑色墨水在白色纸上记录了它论文。他的证明没有发出蓝光，也没有在烧杯捕捉到克里斯的光芒的那一刻颤动。但它是诚实和正确的。他对克里斯的敬意。

Although Alan is agitated by his own faith—a faith that has never crystallized as well as he had hoped—he does not allow his spiritual leniency to corrupt his pure view of mathematics. As a tribute to Morcom, Turing analyzed sulfur dioxide and iodic acid in explicit mathematical detail. Beneath the differential equations and the chemical compositions he found a sharp result. Lucid and true. He recorded it in black ink on white paper. His proof did not glow in blue or throb with the thrill of the moment the beaker trapped Chris's radiance. But it was honest and right. His homage to Chris.

他在日记中写信给克里斯，在日记中他编造了关于化学模型的论点，或者自动化机器的蓝图，或者关于 K. 哥德尔不完备定理的辩论。他和克里斯本可以像这样一起度过几天，彻夜畅谈，休息时下盘棋。克里斯在谢尔本移动车的记忆让艾伦感到安心。克里斯做出了选择。他行使了自由意志。但这么多年过去了，艾伦的脑海里仍然有一个刺，他的信仰中有一个陷阱。克里斯遵守规则。他执行了一项简单的机械任务。他应用了一些方法。一种计算。

He writes to Chris in his diary, where he invents arguments about chemical models, or a blueprint for an automated machine, or a debate over K. Gödel's incompleteness theorems. He and Chris could have spent days together like this, talking all night, taking breaks with a game of chess. Alan is reassured by the memory of Chris shifting a rook back at Sherborne. Chris made a choice. He exercised free will. But still after all these years there remains a barb in Alan's head, a snare in his faith. Chris adhered to the rules. He executed a simple mechanical task. He applied some method. A computation.

艾伦仰面躺在茂盛的草地上，望着灰色的天空，思考着选择。他思考着规则。思考着方法。思考着机械化。他想知道思想本身是否可以机械化。大脑由机械过程建模——由机器建模。他想知道机器是否可以思考。

Lying on his back in the lush grass with a view of gray sky, Alan thinks about choice. He thinks about rules. About method. About mechanization. He wonders if thought itself can be mechanized. The brain modeled by a mechanical process—by a machine. He wonders if a machine could think.

他把棋盘的方格画在一本小小的、略微潮湿的笔记本上，放在他的后口袋里。然后他把棋盘重新画成一条长条，把方块展开成一条带子——这是无限方格带的开始。他在方格中画上 0 和 1，然后划掉它们，再重新开始，做一些小的修改。他用数字的模式编码规则。然后他画了一台读取带子的机器。他不是一个优秀的绘图员，所以这台机器被擦掉了​​好几次，换成了一个简单的盒子插图。他的机器的有限状态将对 0 和 1 的模式做出反应，并按照它们的指令行事。

He draws the squares of a chessboard in a small, slightly damp notebook he keeps in his back pocket. Then he redraws the board as a strip, unfolding the blocks into a tape—the beginning of an infinite tape of squares. He draws 0s and 1s into the squares, crossing them out and starting again with small corrections. He encodes rules in the patterns of numbers. Then he draws a machine that reads the tape. He is not a fine draftsman so the machine is rubbed out several times and replaced with a simple illustration of a box. The finite states of his machine will respond to the pattern of 0s and 1s, will do as they instruct.

夜幕降临之前，他仰面躺着，在露水中感到一阵冰冷，他看到了国际象棋如何实现机械化，因为他清楚地看到了如何将 1 + 1 机械化。他发明了一台可以加法的机器。一台没有思想、没有精神、没有灵魂的机器。

Before the late hours of night, still on his back, growing cold in the dew, he sees how chess might be mechanized because he sees exactly how to mechanize 1 + 1. He invents a machine that can add. A machine with no mind, no spirit, no soul.

他的发现在他的思想中闪闪发光。他头脑中系统探索的这颗宝石透过头顶旋转的穹顶投射出刺眼的金光。他冒着夜深人静的寒冷，忠实地注视着真理的光芒，然后跑回家，丢掉了他的苹果，苹果从口袋里掉出来，逐渐腐烂在草地上。

His discovery shines in his thoughts. This jewel of his mind's systematic expedition casts piercing rays of gold through the rotating dome above him. Rising into the outright cold of the early night he stares faithfully at truth's gleam as he runs home, losing his apple, which falls from his pocket and gradually rots into the matter of the meadow.

但是 1 + 1 = 2 中上帝在哪里呢？

But where is God in 1 + 1 = 2?

艾伦房间的每个小窗户都镶嵌着一个方格，从不同的角度俯瞰宇宙。他试图选择自己最喜欢的景色。有时是国王学院茂密的草坪。有时他能在一个方格内捕捉到穿过草地的整个人，直到这个人走开，然后，就像窗户是一台逃亡相机一样，他试图在图像漂移到他的画面之外之前跟随它。今天，以及自从他在草地上被发现以来的许多天里，他最喜欢的景色一直是纯净的空气。他躺在房间的地板上，用皱巴巴的裤子当枕头，凝视着那块粗糙的画布。他无法忍受看人。他们破坏了他的注意力。所以他只有在跑回格兰切斯特或沿着剑河跑向伦敦时才会离开房间。偶尔，他会在市中心停下来吃一个苹果，瞥一眼商人，他把一箱红色球体放在一个旧桶上。然后他沿着银街跑去，人行道有些地方太窄了，他只能沿着凹凸不平的道路跑到牛栏门前。当他停下来关上身后的栅栏时，他回头看了看小镇的方向，惊叹于自己对这个地方的看法变化得如此之快。他新的世界观在他身后一扫而光，尽可能快地清除了垃圾的混乱，以至于剩下的东西似乎真的闪闪发光。

Each tessellated square of the small windows of Alan's room claims a different angle on the universe. He tries to select his favorite view. Sometimes it is the dense lawn of King's College. Sometimes he can trap a whole person crossing the grass in one square before the person wanders off, and then, as though the window is a fugitive camera, he tries to follow the image before it drifts beyond his frame. Today, and for the many days since his discovery in the meadow, his favorite view has been of pure air. He lies on the floor of his room, using crumpled trousers as a pillow, to contemplate the grainy canvas. He cannot bear to look at people. They destroy his concentration. So he leaves his room only for a run back to Grantchester or a run toward London along the River Cam. Occasionally he will stop in the town center for an apple and a glimpse of the merchant with the one crate of red spheres balanced on an old barrel. Then he runs down Silver Street, the sidewalk so narrow in places that he takes the uneven road the short stretch to the cattle gate. When he stops to close the fence behind him, he looks back in the direction of the town and marvels at how quickly his perception of the place is altering. His new worldview sweeps behind him and cleans away rubbishy confusion as fast as he can run so that what's left actually seems to glint.

当他穿过羊草地时，他避开了别人的目光。人们需要的关注会扰乱他的思绪。一旦激动起来，他就很难回到自己的思想中去。他穿过田野，直到他感到自己完全孤身一人。他靠在弯曲的膝盖上，双臂伸直，调整呼吸。当他坐在地上时，他思考着数字。关于 1、3 和π。机器可以计算这些数字。指令很简单，他可以将指令本身编码成数字。

As he runs through Sheep's Green, he avoids other eyes. The attention people require is disruptive. Once agitated, he finds it difficult to make his way back to the privacy of his mind. He runs through the fields until he feels he is completely alone. He catches his breath while leaning into straight arms balanced on bent knees. As he settles on the ground he thinks about numbers. About 1 and 3 and π. A machine could compute these numbers. The instructions would be simple and he could encode the instructions themselves into numbers.

日复一日，他躺在房间的地板上，凝视着从左边数第二个、从上往下数第二个棋盘格中夹着的颗粒状空气。日复一日，他穿过田野，离伟大的想法越来越近了。

Day after day he lies on the floor of his room staring into the piece of granulated air caught in the second tessellated square from the left and second from the top. Day after day he runs through the fields and he gets closer to the great idea.

他想到了机器。遵循代码编写的规则的机器。他的机器可以加、除和计算数字，一个无限的数字列表。当他在脑海中整理可计算数字列表时，他惊讶地想告诉克里斯，在图书馆抓住他或在课堂上对他耳语，在纸上为他画出这些数字，向他展示一个惊人的事实：有一些实数不在列表中。这个列表是无限的，但还不够无限。他发现了另一个无限的数字列表——无法用任何规则定义的数字。一个试图计算这些数字的机器永远不会停止。他发现了一个无法证明的数学事实的无限列表。无法计算的数字永远超出了人类的理性。

He thinks of machines. Machines that follow rules written in code. His machines can add and divide and compute numbers, an infinite list of numbers. As he sorts the list of computable numbers in his mind, in his amazement, he longs to tell Chris, to catch him in the library or whisper to him during class, to sketch the numbers on paper for him, to show him the remarkable truth: There are real numbers that are not on the list. The list is infinite, but not infinite enough. He finds another infinite list of numbers—numbers that cannot be defined by any rule. A machine that tries to compute them will never halt. He finds an infinite list of mathematical facts that can never be proven. Uncomputable numbers forever beyond human reason.

他感到不舒服和烦躁，满脑子都是想法。有些东西让他很烦躁。随着这些令人难以置信的顿悟，他的物质主义不断升级，而他笨拙的信仰却让他感到厌烦、抱怨和痛苦。他的物质主义与他的信仰。艾伦带着一种病态的迷恋，看着他的信仰被残酷地剥去，带着怜悯和对失败者的蔑视。他自己精心设计的精神框架堆积在物质元素上，就像一座脆弱的纸牌屋，他很容易就把它炸毁了。

He is uncomfortable and irritable, bloated with ideas. And something abrades him. His materialism escalates with these incredible epiphanies even as his awkward faith cloys and whines and nags him into misery. His materialism versus his faith. With a kind of morbid fascination, Alan stares at the brutal flaying of his beliefs with pity and a smudge of contempt for the loser. His own elaborate framework of spirit stacked on elements of matter, a frail house of cards that he easily blows apart.

人类的思想也可以简化为一台机器。当他在草地上奔跑、在树林里奔跑、在桥梁上奔跑、在牛群中穿梭时，这个想法驱动着所有其他想法。思想状态可以被机器状态所取代。人类思维可以分解为简单的规则，机器可以遵循的指令。思想可以被机械化。这种联系并不十分清楚，但它确实存在，是一块巨大晶体的催化剂。不仅仅是思想可以被机械化。它已经机械化了。大脑是一台机器。一台生物机器。这个想法让他从头到脚都冷静下来，一股理解的浪潮洗净了他的困惑、混乱的观念和呼吸。震惊的感觉是这样的：没有天空或大地。没有时间，没有意义。这是一种悸动——一种强烈的沉默，一种脉搏。它无色、无味、无意义。一场白热的爆炸。

The human mind can also be reduced to a machine. This idea drives all the others as he runs on grass, past trees, over bridges, through cattle. States of the mind can be replaced by states of the machine. Human thought can be broken down into simple rules, instructions a machine can follow. Thought can be mechanized. The connection isn’t perfectly clear, but it is there, the catalyst of a great crystal. It is not just that thought can be mechanized. It is mechanized. The brain is a machine. A biological machine. The idea cools him from head to toe, a wave of understanding washing clean his confusion, his muddled notions, and his breath. Shock feels like this: There is no sky or earth. No time, no meaning. It's a throb—a hard silence, a pulse. It is colorless, tasteless, senseless. A white-hot explosion.

巨浪摧毁了他的信仰。他沿着剑河奔跑，踩踏着他悼念克里斯的熟悉地点。当他完全承认这一点时，他的步伐平稳、轻松、自信：1+1=2 中的上帝在哪里？无处不在。

The wave crushes away his faith. He runs along the Cam, trampling familiar spots where he mourned Chris. His stride is smooth and easy and confident when the admission forms completely: Where is God in 1+1 = 2? Nowhere.

在接下来的几天、几周和几个月里，他感觉自己最后的灵性就像发烧后冷却的残留物一样消失了。他毫无悔意，想知道自己怎么会如此激动地坚持自己那笨拙的信仰。我们只是机器。在二十三岁和余生里，他毫无保留地接受了独立于我们而存在的数学——尽管我们与数学相反，并不能独立生活。我们是生物机器。仅此而已。我们没有灵魂，没有精神。但我们与数学息息相关，而数学是完美的。这必须是真的。

Over the coming days and weeks and on into months, he feels the last of his spiritualism evaporate like the cooling remains of a fever. He is left without remorse and wonders how he ever clung to his awkward faith with such emotion. We’re just machines. At the age of twenty-three and for the rest of his life he embraces, without reservation, a mathematics that exists independently of us—although we, by contrast, do not live independently of it. We are biological machines. Nothing more. We have no souls, no spirit. But we are bound to mathematics and mathematics is flawless. This has to be true.

1 + 1 = 2 中上帝在哪里？没有上帝。

Where is God in 1 + 1 =2? There is no God.

抛开信仰的过滤，回首往事，他开始接受多年前预感的真正含义——蓝色水坑中碎裂的玻璃烧杯——那种恶臭恐惧的真正来源，不是克里斯的死亡，而是信仰的消亡。这不是预感，不是超越时间界限或命运的指示。这是一种指控，是指向他自欺欺人的手指。恐惧帮助他否认它，扼杀现实主义的精髓，躲在幼稚的愿望背后，寻求童话故事的解药。但这种不适持续了这么多年，直到他终于放弃了对唯心论的防御。他并没有神秘地直觉到克里斯快要死了。他清楚地目睹了克里斯的衰落。他看到了克里斯明显瘦削的脖子和突出的锁骨。他食欲不振。他压抑的不适和可能的疼痛。艾伦当时害怕的是，当克里斯离开时，他就走了，而他现在知道这是真的。克里斯去世后，艾伦的信仰也开始动摇。当信仰最终消失时，它就消失了。

Looking back without the filter of faith, he comes to accept that the real meaning of his premonition all those years ago—a shattered glass beaker in a puddle of blue—the actual source of that foul dread, was not Chris's death, but the death of faith. It wasn’t a premonition, a step over time's boundary or fate's dictates. It was an accusation, a finger pointing at his self-deception. Fear helped him deny it, choke down the nugget of realism, hide behind childish wishes for the remedy of fairy tales. But the discomfort lasted all these years until he finally let his defensive grip on spiritualism fall away. He did not mystically intuit that Chris was dying. He plainly witnessed his decline. He saw Chris's conspicuously gaunt neck and protruding clavicles. His poor appetite. His suppressed discomfort and probable pain. What Alan feared then, and what he now knows to be true, is that when Chris was gone, he was gone. When Chris died, so began the decline of Alan's faith. And when it was finally gone, it was gone.

令他惊讶的是，他的物质主义并没有那么悲哀。他更加轻松、更有韧性，恐惧感更少。仿佛他的眼睛突然从令人作呕的模糊中恢复过来，整个世界都变得明亮起来。他所有的感官都变得敏锐，以至于颜色、声音、气味和质地都变得绚丽而生动，他对它们的体验是一种令人心旷神怡的喜悦。每一片草叶都闪闪发光。剑桥的强风吹得他心生敬畏。每一棵树的每一根树枝的荒芜扭曲，甚至微弱的光雾，整个世界都在向他歌唱，仿佛他以前从未见过或听过任何东西。随着这种触觉觉醒的纯粹乐趣，他对大自然的热爱愈演愈烈，仿佛他终于完全、毫无顾忌地臣服于她。几天之内，他的精神主义就只不过是一个有点尴尬的、幼稚的记忆。取而代之的是一种平静、无动于衷的物质主义——与他所担心的悲伤、凄凉的空虚完全不同。他很难用语言表达出来。没有一个词可以表达这种感觉。他必须写一篇很长的文章，里面有很多警告和离题，即使这样，他也知道他无法成功地表达这种直观体验的即时性或壮丽性。也许在别人的脑海中会有更好的词，但在他心中却不会。所以他的脑海里给了他一些简单的东西。只有一个词反复出现在他脑海中，而且可能只有这个——一个他经常不会想到要用的词：“美丽”。它很美。

To his great surprise his materialism is not so sad. He moves with greater ease and resilience and less fear. It is as though his eyes have suddenly come into focus from a nauseating blur and the whole world looks brilliant. All of his senses have sharpened so that colors and sounds and smells and textures are splendid and vibrant, his experience of them a heart-soaring joy. Every blade of grass glistens. The hard Cambridge wind batters respect out of him. The barren twist of every branch of every tree, even the weak fog of light, the whole of the world sings out to him as though he has never seen or heard anything before. With the sheer pleasure of this tactile awakening, his love of nature intensifies as though he has finally given over to her, wholly and without inhibition. Within days his spiritualism is no more than a mildly embarrassing, childish memory. In its place is a calm, impervious materialism—nothing like the sad, bleak emptiness he feared. He would have a bad time trying to put it into words. No single word could mean this thing. He would have to write something lengthy with many caveats and tangents and even then he knows he would not successfully express the immediacy or the splendor of the visceral experience. Maybe in another's mind better words would come, but not in his. And so his mind offers him something simple. Only one word comes to him over and again, and it could be only this—a word he doesn’t often think to use: “beautiful.” It is beautiful.

维也纳，1936 年

VIENNA, 1936

这是库尔特一生中最糟糕的一年。他被送进了疗养院。他不确定自己是怎么来到这里的。严格地说，阿黛尔开着一辆借来的车把他送到了维也纳一百英里外的地方。他弯着腰从前门走进来，带着一个恳求的面具登记入住。在他签字之前，阿黛尔已经只是沙砾中的一个法医痕迹，她已经回到了城里。这就是他来到这里的原因。但更深层次的原因，因果关系，很难解开。

The worst year of Kurt's life. He has committed himself to a sanatorium. He isn’t sure how he got here. Strictly speaking, Adele drove him the hundred miles outside Vienna in a borrowed car. He hunched through the front doors and with an imploring mask checked himself in. Before his signature was applied to paper, Adele was already only a forensic mark in the gravel, well on her way back to the city. That's how he got here. But the deeper reasons, the dominoes of cause and effect, they are difficult to disentangle.

他为自己讲述的解释自己人生轨迹的故事中，有一些决定性因素。没有一个故事和几个关键点，他的生活就只是一串不完整的记忆，他甚至不相信这些记忆的准确性，甚至不相信他实际的经历。他从少数几个决定性因素中推断出自己的历史。有因必有果。但故事因不准确而被扭曲。他需要一个清晰而明确的计算力量和后果，但这种计算的复杂性甚至超出了他的能力。如果他无法证明答案，他怎么知道他是否自由？他是否可以自由地做出与此刻不同的选择？他是否可以自由地选择吃面包皮，或者他唯一可能做出的选择就是这种节制的选择？他不确定自己是否能如此清楚地看到真相。由于没有完美的计算，他被迫做出近似，将创造这一时刻的几乎无限的力量减少到仅仅几个。

There are determining factors in the story he tells himself to explain his trajectory. Without a story with its few key points, his life is a string of incomplete memories that he trusts even less for their accuracy than he did the actual experiences. From a handful of determining factors he draws inferences about his history. There is cause and there is effect. But then stories are distorted by inexactitude. He would need a clean and unambiguous calculation of forces and consequences, but the complexity of such a computation is beyond even him. If he cannot prove the answer, how can he know if he is free? Is he free to choose differently than he has in this moment? Is he free to choose to eat the crust of bread, or is the only choice he could possibly make this abstemious choice? He is not sure he can see the truth quite so plainly. In the absence of a perfect calculation he is forced to make approximations, to reduce down to just a few the nearly infinite forces acting to create this moment.

他把关键因素归纳为五个。首先，他动身前往美国。他尝试了两次。第一次尝试时，他设法乘坐东方快车离开维也纳，当时奥尔加和奥托在站台的一端挥手告别，却没有意识到库尔特的兄弟在另一端挥手告别。当火车驶入下一站时，库尔特已经多次测量体温，确定自己身体不好。他的兄弟发现库尔特拖着行李回到维也纳并要求医生治疗，感到非常恶心。经过几天的哄骗和说服，库尔特和他的火车终于从一个几乎空无一人的站台上出发了，没有朋友和家人的挥手。他到达了英格兰海岸，在那里登上了一艘船，看着陆地的曲线在雾中缩小。在与傲慢的美国游客一起被困了六天之后，他对接受新泽西州普林斯顿高等研究院奖学金的顾虑越来越多，但看到曼哈顿从栏杆上爬上来，他打消了这种顾虑。这些建筑让他燃起了希望。他即将加入知识分子从欧洲前往新大陆的大批移民行列，尽管只是暂时的。这些难民来自伟大的偶像为有前途的人才，他定居在风景秀丽、树木繁茂、与世隔绝的普林斯顿，享有特权和地理位置，免受美国其他地区经济衰退的困扰。也许在美国他会得到他在欧洲得不到的认可。

He has reduced the key factors to a list of five. First, he left for America. It took two attempts. With the initial attempt, he managed to depart Vienna on the Orient Express as Olga and Otto waved good-bye from one end of the platform without realizing Kurt's brother waved good-bye from the other. By the time the train pulled into the next station Kurt had taken his temperature several times and determined he was poorly. His brother was entirely disgusted to find Kurt had returned to Vienna dragging his luggage and requesting a doctor's attention. After a few days of coddling and convincing, Kurt and his train finally pulled away from a nearly empty platform, absent of waving friends and family. He made it to the coast of England, where he boarded a boat and watched the curve of land shrink into the fog. After he had spent six days trapped with brash American tourists, his mounting reservations over accepting a fellowship at the Institute for Advanced Study in Princeton, New Jersey, were dispelled by the sight of Manhattan climbing over the railings. His hope rose with those buildings. He was about to join, if only temporarily, a mass exodus of intellectuals from Europe to the New World. The refugees, from great icons to promising talents, settled in a pretty, wooded, and insulated Princeton protected by privilege and geography from the privations of an economic depression dehydrating the rest of the United States. Maybe in America he would find the recognition that eluded him in Europe.

他仍在等待认可。他像一个需要服用救命药的慢性病患者一样等待。在他的论文《论数学原理和相关系统的形式不可判定命题》发表后，他在一次数学会议上宣布了他的研究成果，但大多数人都忽视了他，少数人误解了他，少数人攻击了他。有一位来自普林斯顿的冯·诺依曼教授在他的研讨会结束后把他拉到一边，他们聊得好像他们的生命都依赖于此。他们谈论素数和唯一因式分解，将关于数字的陈述编码为数字；关于证明；甚至关于真理。不久之后，高等研究院就邀请他到普林斯顿大学担任短期研究员。在莫里茨那可怕的恼怒表情和最终的严重失望之后，库尔特自己的失望已经成为一种顽固的负担。他再也没有和莫里茨谈过他的不完全性定理，尽管他们偶尔会交谈，但事情再也回不到从前了。这种失望总是萦绕在他们心头。成绩公布后不久，库尔特的情绪就变得低落，他需要去医院短暂休息。至少他的母亲和哥哥在 1931 年将他送进医院度过那几个月的痛苦时光时认为这是必要的。因此，当普林斯顿大学的邀请函寄来时，他在欧洲感到很疏远，于是接受了。

He is still waiting for that recognition. He waits like a chronically ill patient requiring life-preserving medication. After his paper “On Formally Undecidable Propositions of Principia Mathematica and Related Systems” was published, he announced his results at a mathematical conference and was ignored by most, misunderstood by a handful, attacked by another handful. There was one man, a Professor von Neumann from Princeton, who pulled him aside after his seminar, and they talked as if their very lives depended on it. They talked about prime numbers and unique factorizations, coding statements about numbers into numbers; about proof; and even about truth. It wasn’t long before an invitation came from the Institute for Advanced Study for a short-term fellowship in Princeton. After that terrible look of irritation and then finally grave disappointment from Moritz, Kurt's own disappointment had become an obstinate encumbrance. He never spoke to Moritz of his incompleteness theorems again and though they did speak from time to time things were never the same. It always hung about them. Shortly after his results solidified, Kurt's mood darkened and a short rest in an institution had been necessary. At least his mother and brother believed it necessary when they committed him for those few terrible months back in 1931. So when the invitation to Princeton arrived, feeling estranged in Europe, he accepted.

在普林斯顿研究所的第一天，他遇到了至少一位熟悉的面孔，那就是经济学家奥斯卡·摩根斯坦，几年前莫里茨将他介绍给了维也纳学派。奥斯卡因纳粹认为其卑鄙的政治立场而被免去奥地利经济研究所所长一职，之后他在美国寻求庇护。他在高等研究院成为一名有成就的学者，虽然感谢新世界提供的庇护，但他常常为祖国被疾病侵蚀的灵魂感到痛心。在研究所的第一天早上，哥德尔走进摆满期刊和穿着舒适的学者的咖啡室，奥斯卡坐在那里读《纽约时报》。奥斯卡急切地想听到他麻风病患者的消息。“奥地利的情况怎么样？”他问道，脸上带着担忧。“咖啡太难喝了。是什么把你带到美国的，摩根斯坦先生？”库尔特的回答令人震惊。当话题转到鬼魂时，奥斯卡惊呆了，并没有感到被冒犯，反而惊叹于新朋友的“超凡​​脱俗”。

On his first day at the institute in Princeton he was greeted by at least one familiar face, the economist Oskar Morgenstern, whom Moritz had introduced to the Vienna Circle several years earlier. Oskar sought refuge in America after his brisk dismissal as the director of the Austrian Institute for Economic Research owing to what the Nazis characterized as his despicable politics. He established himself as an accomplished scholar at the Institute for Advanced Study and though grateful for the shelter provided by the New World, he often ached for the disease-rotted soul of his own country. On his first morning at the institute, Gödel entered the coffee room lined with journals and comfortably dressed scholars and there sat Oskar reading The New York Times. Oskar was anxious to hear news of his leprous home. “And how are things in Austria?” he asked, contorted with concern. “The coffee is wretched. What brings you to America, Herr Morgenstern?” was Kurt's astounding reply. When the conversation then turned to ghosts, Oskar was too flabbergasted to feel affronted and marveled instead at his new friend's “otherworldliness.”

“这太奇怪了，太好笑了，太好笑了，几乎可以原谅，”他告诉妻子，然后转达了那句令人惊奇的回答：“咖啡太难喝了。”虽然其他一些欧洲人对库尔特对纳粹国内麻烦的轻率、含糊其辞的态度感到不快，但奥斯卡确实原谅了他，两人结下了友谊。库尔特至少有了一个朋友，有了一些新想法，对自己在美国的未来总体上持乐观态度，因此精神状态非常好。甚至在新朋友、新想法和新想法出现之前，库尔特的乐观态度十分合理，从他看到曼哈顿耸立在船栏杆上的那一刻起，他的情绪就一直高涨。这种好心情一直持续了将近一个月。

“It was so odd it was funny, so funny it was almost forgivable,” he told his wife after he relayed the remarkable reply: “The coffee is wretched.” While some of the other Europeans were offended by Kurt's airy, noncommittal attitude toward the Nazi troubles at home, Oskar did indeed forgive him and a friendship was forged. With at least one friend, some new ideas, and a general optimism about his future in America, Kurt was in very good spirits. Even before the new friend, the ideas, and his reasonable optimism, Kurt's spirits were high from that moment when he saw Manhattan loom over the railing of the boat. The good mood lasted until nearly an entire month had passed.

导致他在疗养院入口处签名的因果关系中的第二个事件更加模糊。它发生在该研究所每周举行的一次原本很普通的座谈会上。他坐在一把典型的木制学校椅子上，这把椅子被螺栓固定在坚实的地板上，靠近通往讲台和绿色黑板框架的陡峭狭窄台阶的顶部。它看起来像一座黄色的小山，木椅代替了干草，人们随意地在座位之间穿梭。哥德尔坐在一排空座位的外侧。他瘦弱的双腿交叉在身前，随意地形成一道倾斜的屏障，阻止任何人挤进他旁边的空座位。

The second event in the falling dominoes of cause and effect that led him to sign the book at the entrance of the sanatorium was more nebulous. It developed during an otherwise entirely average colloquium held weekly at the institute. He sat in a typical wooden school chair that was bolted to the solid floor near the top of the steep narrow steps that led to the podium and the frame of green chalkboards. It looked like a yellow hill with wooden chairs in place of dry grass, with people tossed carelessly among the seats. Gödel took a place on the outside of an empty row. His thin legs, crossed in front of him, created a casual, angled barrier discouraging anyone from pushing into the empty stretch of seats alongside him.

他觉得这些座谈会很丢脸。每个人都嘲笑演讲者的拙劣笑话。在正式开场白之前，每个人都在傻乎乎地闲聊——就像老朋友一样。他们似乎都在合作，在解决同样的问题，陷入同样的​​潮流中，而他却停滞不前，看着他们欢快地飘过。研究所的年轻人很容易晋升，而他却感到孤立无援。他通常不会心怀怨恨，但喋喋不休的谈话、研讨会主题和演讲者的统一性、学术单位的凝聚力所代表的光明而雄心勃勃的世界，放大了他的孤立和不安全感。最糟糕的是，默契和其他人在一起——没有他，团队自然而然地组织起来——放大了一种非常接近失败的感觉，以至于他的胃酸沸腾，胆汁翻腾。就在那时，他深深地绝望地意识到，他永远无法融入这里。

He found these colloquia humiliating. Everyone laughed at the speaker's poor jokes. Everyone chatted foolishly before the formal opening remarks—chatted like old friends. They all seemed to be collaborating, working on the same problems, caught in the same flow, while he stagnated and watched them float merrily past. Young recruits to the institute advanced easily while he felt isolated, invisible. He wasn’t generally given to bitterness, but the shiny ambitious world represented in the chirping conversations, the uniformity of seminar topics and speakers, the cohesiveness of the academic unit, magnified his isolation and his insecurity. Worst of all, the tacit agreement among the others—the team that naturally organized without him—amplified a feeling terribly close to failure so that his stomach acids bubbled and his bile churned. And just then he realized with deep despair and certainty that he would never quite fit in here.

最初，他刚抵达新大陆时还抱有乐观的态度，但孤独感让他更加迷失了方向，这反而让他更加堕落。他什么都不是，什么都不是，什么都不是。他只是美国的一个临时学者，每年领着津贴。没有永久职位。欧洲没有，普林斯顿也没有。他强迫自己承认，他假装不需要同事的尊重，但这种尊重并没有到来。他尴尬得瑟瑟发抖。他用双手捂住垂下的头，安慰了自己几分钟，然后直起身子回到房间里，仿佛才想起他现在所处的环境，以及研讨会上聚集的难以忍受的成功之光。

His initial optimism on arriving in the New World only served to increase the height of his fall as isolation disoriented him. He was nothing, nobody, insignificant. A temporary scholar in America on a year-by-year stipend. No permanent position on offer. Not in Europe, not in Princeton. He forced himself to admit that the respect from his colleagues that he pretended not to need was not forthcoming. He cringed with embarrassment. He covered his hanging head with both hands to comfort himself for several minutes before straightening back into the room as if just remembering his current surroundings, the unbearable brightness of success gathered for the colloquium.

走在普林斯顿的新鲜空气中，他从这种不安的悲伤中得到了一些喘息，但当他面对着孤独的住所中的单人间和公用厕所时，这种缓解是短暂的。那天晚上，在座谈会和满屋子穿着毛衣的成功人士之后，他躺在床上，屈服于这种感觉，这是他接近下降轨道的第三个事件，比第二个事件更具决定性——一种迅速而彻底的抑郁。这是发自内心的，一点也不抽象。他比感冒的明确开始更准确地感觉到它的开始。首先，他的新陈代谢开始下降。他的血压下降，呼吸变慢，肌肉松弛。他滑入了一种荒凉的状态，如此干燥，他没有了热情抽泣。但在下山的路上，有几分钟他感觉很踏实，就像他想象中其他人一定习惯的感觉一样。这只是他下山途中短暂的停顿，让他可以瞥见他经过前的风景——不太明亮，不太戏剧化，但也不是令人讨厌的平淡。只是清脆、清晰和可以忍受。然后，暗流把他拉了起来，他重重地沉入粘稠的瘴气中，压力如此巨大，他想知道为什么没有人能看到他痛苦的本质。

His walk home in the fresh Princeton air offered some respite from this restless sadness, but the relief was short-lived when he faced his single room and the shared toilet in his painfully lonely lodging. That night after the colloquium and the roomful of sweater-covered achievers, he lay prone in his bed and gave in to it, the third event in his approximation to his descending orbit, more decisive than the second—a swift and thorough depression. It was visceral and not at all abstract. He felt its onset more precisely than the unambiguous beginning of a head cold. First his metabolism ramped down. His blood pressure ebbed, his breathing slowed, and his muscles slackened. Down he slid into a desolation so arid he didn’t have the enthusiasm to sob. But on the decline there were a few minutes of solidity when he felt just as he imagined other people must habitually feel. It was only a momentary pause during his descent, a rest allowing him a glimpse of the landscape before he passed by—not too bright, not too dramatic, but not hatefully bland either. Just crisp and clear and bearable. Then the undertow yanked him and he sank heavily in a viscous miasma, the pressure so imposing, he wondered why no one else could see the substance of his misery.

在接下来的几周里，甚至连他结交的几个朋友都被迫安全地退后，因为抑郁像一场迟来的火灾穿过干旱的森林一样席卷了他。尤其是奥斯卡，他发现自己无助地对库尔特的疯狂说三道四，就像在火焰风暴中用一双手浇灌一捧无力的水。美国医生在他即将精神崩溃时将他送回维也纳。他们建议把他带回家。把他封闭起来。让他和一个家庭说德语，这个家庭可能会在大坝崩溃和偏执淹没他之前把他拉回来。他在普林斯顿只做了两个月的研究员，但抗议是没有用的。他身体不好。他需要回家。他需要阿黛尔。所以他放弃了津贴、租来的房间和对物质的最后尝试。

Over the coming weeks, even the few friends he had made were forced to stand safely back as depression tore through him like an overdue fire through a parched forest. Oskar in particular found himself helplessly throwing platitudes on Kurt's madness like feeble handfuls of water on a storm of flames. American doctors sent him back to Vienna with a nervous collapse imminent. Get him home, they advised. Seal him in. Let him speak German with a family that might hold him together before the dam breaks and the paranoia drowns him. He was only two months into his fellowship in Princeton, but there was no use protesting. He wasn’t well. He needed to be home. He needed Adele. So he gave up his stipend, his rented room, and a last tentative hold on materiality.

科特乘坐尚普兰号出发，周五横渡大西洋，几天后抵达勒阿弗尔。从那里，他将前往巴黎，然后转乘火车前往维也纳，与他的兄弟、母亲和他的秘密情人阿黛尔团聚。奥斯卡对登上船的瘦削身影大声说道：“你会没事的。”但他知道这是个谎言，于是迅速转身违背诺言。他匆忙给科特的兄弟发了这封电报：

Kurt departed on the Champlain, crossing the Atlantic on Friday to arrive in Le Havre within several days. From there he would travel to Paris before connecting to a train to Vienna—to his brother, to his mother, to his secret Adele. Oskar said aloud to the gaunt figure boarding the boat, “You’ll be okay.” But he knew this to be a lie and turned quickly to break his promise. He scrambled to send this telegram to Kurt's brother:

哥德尔博士乘坐尚普兰号返回欧洲。停。患有神经疾病。停。担心最坏的情况。停。

Dr. Gödel returns to Europe aboard the Champlain. STOP. Suffering nervous condition. STOP. Fear the worst. STOP.

他口述电报时强调了每一个“停止”的字眼，希望自己能对码头上那个神经衰弱的人发出同样的命令。停止。停止。停止。看在上帝的份上，停下来。

As he dictated the telegram he emphasized each “STOP” wishing he could blurt the same command to the nervous wreck he had left on the dock. Stop. Stop. Stop. For Chrissake, stop.

第四件事是横渡大西洋的旅程，这简直是一场生动的恐怖，黑色的漩涡将他卷上岸，他是一个精神错乱的被抛弃者，脱离了现实。这次令人晕船的旅程不需要再多说什么了，除了他的哥哥把他留在巴黎，无视奥斯卡的电报和库尔特的恳求电话。他躲在火车站附近一家只有单人间的小旅馆里，害怕桌子后面的女孩，害怕她悬挂在钥匙上的轮廓，害怕一场冰冷的黄铜雨。他想死，但对保存肉体的解剖学痴迷是一种更深的诱惑——至少在一段时间内是这样。微妙的平衡倾向于生命，但并不稳定。他的自杀将是缓慢的、痛苦的、难以想象的。这将是这两种驱动力找到同等力量的唯一可能结果：生存的决心和死亡的决心之间的最终僵局。

The fourth event was the trip across the Atlantic, nothing short of a vivid horror, a black engulfing swirl that spat him up on shore, a deranged castaway broken from reality. No more needs to be reported of that sea-sickening voyage except that his brother left him in Paris, ignoring Oskar's telegram and Kurt's pleading phone calls. He hid in a small hotel of single rooms near the train station, terrified of the girl behind the desk, of her silhouette against the hanging keys, a frozen rainfall of brass. He wanted to die but the anatomical obsession with the preservation of his mortal flesh was an even deeper seduction—at least for a while. The delicate balance tipping in favor of life, but unstably. His suicide will be slow, excruciating, unimaginable. It will be the only possible outcome of these two drives finding equal strength: the ultimate impasse between the determination to live and the determination to die.

经过三天的失败尝试，他终于登上了前往维也纳的火车。阿黛尔在站台上等着他，双手捧着一个手提包。“来吧，库尔特勒。我做了汤。”尽管他已经精神崩溃，但还是在阿黛尔的照顾下度过了几个月，直到第五次也是最糟糕的一次——莫里茨被谋杀。

After three days of failed attempts, he managed to board a train to Vienna. Adele waited for him on the station platform, holding a handbag in front of her with two hands. “Come, Kurtele. I’ve made soup.” Even though he was criminally dispirited, he coped for some months under Adele's care before the fifth and worst event. Moritz's murder.

莫里茨去世那天早上，比平时晚出门，他一边和妻子告别，一边蹦蹦跳跳地走出门外。他被维特根斯坦的危机所困扰。由于前一天晚上在一场激烈的讨论中措辞不当，他几乎完全疏远了维特根斯坦，并花了半夜的大部分时间来抚平两人之间开始出现的裂痕。尽管睡眠不足、起床晚、错过了早餐，莫里茨还是很感激在维特根斯坦几乎完全隐居的几个月里，他是唯一一个可以继续与维特根斯坦交流的人。然后，有些日子，他无法联系到，他的愤怒和他的想法一起被隐藏起来。在那些时候，莫里茨可能会在他们开会的时候紧张地盯着维特根斯坦的背影，而维特根斯坦则在他家族华丽的豪宅中的一个大客厅里面朝墙壁朗诵诗歌（印度诗人泰戈尔的神秘作品）。莫里茨是一个聪明而自信的人，但他私下里却感到尴尬显然，他忽略了一些基本点。维特根斯坦曾经说过：“你让我想起一个人透过一扇关闭的窗户往外看，却无法向自己解释路人奇怪的动作。他不知道外面正刮着什么样的风暴，也许这个人只是费了很大的劲才站稳脚跟。”

The morning of his death, Moritz left his house later than usual, skipping backward out the door as he called good-bye to his wife. He was waylaid by a Wittgenstein crisis. Having chosen his words poorly in a heated discussion the previous evening, he almost alienated the man entirely and spent a good part of the middle of the night smoothing over the beginnings of a rift. Despite the loss of sleep, the late start, the missed breakfast, Moritz was grateful to be the one person Wittgenstein continued to address during his months of near total seclusion. Then there were days when he was unreachable, when his fury was hidden, along with his ideas. At those times, Moritz might spend their meetings staring nervously at his back while Wittgenstein faced the wall in one of the grand sitting rooms in his family's ornate mansion to recite poetry (mystical writings, no less, by the Indian poet Rabindranath Tagore). Moritz, an otherwise clever and confident man, was privately embarrassed by the obvious condition of missing some fundamental point. Wittgenstein once said, “You remind me of someone who is looking through a closed window and cannot explain to himself the strange movements of a passerby. He doesn’t know what kind of storm is raging outside and that this person is perhaps only with great effort keeping himself on his feet.”

莫里茨对维特根斯坦如此着迷，以至于他疯狂地穿越维也纳，寻找任何可能与他会面的机会，而这位哲学家则随意更换地点，不顾通勤的繁重。莫里茨经常带着他的助手，一个薪水微薄的学者，将他的家人安置在弗鲁赫特街的一套两居室公寓里，楼上是一个捷克五口之家，位于多瑙河运河另一侧拥挤的犹太区，莫里茨只能眼睁睁地看着他被羞辱。尽管维特根斯坦有禁欲主义的倾向——比如独自住在挪威的棚屋里，教农村孩子语法，用各种方式摆脱家庭的巨额财富——但他仍然对莫里茨的助手和他的赤贫怀有强烈的蔑视，他没有试图隐藏或淡化这种厌恶。维特根斯坦的犹太祖先几代之前就皈依了天主教，他曾两次向感到不舒服的莫里茨嘲笑道：“这个犹太人真的会修行吗？”

Moritz was so enamored of Wittgenstein that he found himself traveling insanely across Vienna for any possible meeting with him as the philosopher changed venues at random without concern for the burdensome commute it might demand. Moritz often brought his assistant, a poorly paid scholar who kept his family housed in a two-room flat on Fruchtgasse above a Czech family of five in the crowded Jewish quarter on the wrong side of the Danube Canal, only to watch him be humiliated. Despite Wittgenstein's ascetic tendencies—such as living alone in a shack in Norway, teaching rural children grammar, shedding his family's obscene wealth in a myriad of ways—he still held an astringent contempt for Moritz's assistant and his abject poverty, a disgust he made no attempt to hide or soften. Wittgenstein's Jewish ancestors converted to Catholicism generations before, and twice he scoffed to an uncomfortable Moritz, “Does this Jew actually practice?”

莫里茨独自一人或在助手的引诱下，收集着像从男人衬衫袖子上凝结并滴落的露水一样的想法，有时当露水不愿落在他身上时，他还要承担不体面的任务，擦拭掉露水，因此他难以置信地觉得自己像个男仆。

Alone or with the decoy of his assistant, Moritz collected ideas like dew that condensed and dripped from the man's shirtsleeves, sometimes having the undignified task of mopping the dew off when it was reluctant to fall his way, so he felt, with disbelief, like a kind of manservant.

虽然维特根斯坦对莫里茨乃至整个世界的失望常常爆发，但莫里茨却忍住了怒火，反复无常的人会表现出他特有的耐心。如果说有什么压力，那也只是以夸张的形式来展示这种内在的伟大耐心，以至于它被扭曲为谄媚。莫里茨看到维特根斯坦不是疯子，也不是恶霸。他饱受折磨，甚至脆弱不堪，有时像蛋壳一样被咬。所以莫里茨设法不被越来越频繁的咬伤。从外表上看，莫里茨一如既往地镇定自若，甚至看起来很开心。所以在他被谋杀的那天，他很镇定，很满足。

While Wittgenstein's frustration with Moritz and indeed the entire world often flared wildly, Moritz endured the rages and the fickle turns with characteristic patience. If any pressure was apparent, it was only in the form of an exaggerated show of this intrinsically great patience so that it was distorted in the direction of sycophancy. Moritz saw that Wittgenstein was not a madman, nor a bully. He was tormented, fragile even, sometimes crumpling like an eggshell. And so Moritz managed not to feel hurt by the increasingly frequent bites. Outwardly Moritz was as composed as ever and even seemed happy. And so it was on the day of his murder, he was composed and contented.

那天早上，他像往常一样，把领带打成一个完美的结，系在浆过的领子中间，领带在宽翻领下整齐地折好，再套在一件剪裁合身的背心上。莫里茨迟到了本学期的最后一堂课，他穿过大街，匆匆穿过宏伟的大学入口，兴高采烈地跳上台阶，来到他的演讲厅。莫里茨用他安静单调的声音讲了很多关于因果关系、无限性和自由意志的课程。虽然他的演讲风格可能缺乏魅力，但他坚定、博学、善良。他激发了满屋子的忠诚——除了一个人。那个眼神呆滞的人。“他不自由，”莫里茨想。他惊叹于这一周的准备工作是多么徒劳，演讲计划就像一股随意飘出的雪茄烟雾，愚蠢地对准了狂风。所有事件都已启动，每个事件都引发其他事件，直到这一事件像一块精确校准的时钟的敲击一样强制性地发生，扣动扳机的那一刻就是预先确定的。

That morning, as he had for as many mornings as he could remember, he fastened his tie in an immaculate knot at the middle of his throat between starched collars, a tidy fold beneath broad lapels, layered in turn over a tailored waistcoat. Late for his final lecture of the term, Moritz crossed the Strasse, hurried through the grand university entrance, and cheerily skipped steps up to his lecture hall. In Moritz's quiet monotone, he spent many lectures on causality, infinity, free will. And though he may have been short of charisma in his oratory style, he was solid and informative and kind. He inspired loyalty from the full room—minus one. The one with the dead eyes. “He's not free,” Moritz thought. He marveled at the futility of the week's preparations, the lecture plan like a casual stream of cigar smoke aimed foolishly into a gale. All events are set into motion, each causing others, until this event strikes as compulsory as the strike of a perfectly calibrated clock, the squeeze of the trigger predetermined.

在快步走向教室的路上，他思考着量子力学的作用。在讲座结束时，他是否应该参考量子随机性重新讨论整个问题？

During his brisk walk to class, he wondered about the role of quantum mechanics. At the end of the lecture, should he reopen the entire issue with reference to quantum randomness?

他反对自由意志的论证，即自由意志被决定论所扼杀，论证严密、清晰、令人信服。难道他最后只通过拉扯量子不确定性的线索就彻底解开了这个论证吗？无论如何，机会是一种虚假的希望，是学生们用来分散注意力的空洞的消遣，会推迟他们对事实的接受。机会不是自由意志。

His argument against free will, a throttling of free will at the hands of determinism, was so tight, so clearly and convincingly executed. Should he unravel it so completely just at the end by pulling at the stray thread of quantum indeterminacy? Chance is a false hope anyway, a hollow distraction for the students to cling to, postponing their acceptance of the facts. Chance is not free will.

他跳上楼梯，看着自己的脚，仍旧犹豫不决，惊讶于自己似乎要做出选择。他的黑鞋挡住了他的视线，直到另一双脚遮住了他对地板的视线。仿佛在进行一种流畅的连续思考——他的困境的逻辑结论，他的论点的正确性的证明——他抬起头，面对着那张扭曲的嘴唇、那未打理的头发、那白皙的指关节痛苦地卡在黑色金属枪上，最后得出结论：“他不自由。”

He bounded up the stairs, watching his own feet, still undecided and amazed at how it felt as though he were going to exercise choice. His black shoes held his eyes until another pair of feet eclipsed his view of the floor. As though engaged in a smooth continuous thought—the logical conclusion of his dilemma, the demonstration of the correctness of his arguments—his head rose to face the deranged lips, the un-tended hair, the white knuckles wedged painfully around the dark metal gun, to conclude, “He is not free.”

走到楼梯顶端，他很不高兴看到跟踪者挡住了他的路。一个面目狰狞、憔悴、恶毒的男孩，有着精神分裂症患者的浮华想象力。“我不应该放过我的保镖，”莫里茨想。当他第一次得知他那位精神错乱的学生出现嫉妒幻觉时（莫里茨与一个名叫西尔维娅的学生在幻想中热恋），他试图讲道理、像长辈一样安慰、指导和善意。但尽管他尽了一切善意和努力，袭击者那张丑陋的脸还是没有恢复原状。男孩逐渐变成了一个纳粹青年，但这并没有减轻莫里茨自己的焦虑。男孩的头发剪得很短，在耳朵上方，看起来像是剃成了一整条带子，绕着他的头。其余的头发是直的，从侧面分开，在头上卷成一个宽大的圆筒，一根绳子可以穿过他的头。黑色的头发垂在额头上，在右眉角处保持平衡。他的双眼似乎特别靠近，在眼睑下垂的哑光下，他看起来充满恶意。他可怜地模仿了元首，只是男孩的头发在另一边分开，没有紧紧地固定在头上，而是从缝合处抬起，落在一个圆形的大凸起上。

At the top of the stairs he was unhappy to see his stalker blocking his path. A grim, gaunt, vicious boy with a schizophrenic's florid imagination. “I should not have let my bodyguard go,” Moritz thought. When he had first come to learn of his demented student's jealous hallucination (involving Moritz in a torrid, and imaginary, tryst with a student named Sylvia) he tried reason, avuncular reassurance, guidance, and kindness. But for all his goodwill and earnest effort, the crooked ugly face of his assailant would not realign. The boy's steady mutation into a Nazi youth hardly allayed Moritz's own anxieties. The hair was cut so close above the boy's ears as to seem shaved in a full band around his head. The remainder was straight, parted on the side, and scooped over his head in a broad cylinder allowing a rope of black to hang over his forehead and balance at the corner of his right brow. His eyes seemed inordinately close together and he looked spiteful from under the dumb overhang of his eyelids. His was a pathetic imitation of the Führer, except the boy's hair was parted on the opposite side and wasn’t tacked so tight to the head but lifted out of the suture, landing in a big round swell.

疯狂而尖刻的电话夜复一夜地打扰着莫里茨的家，电话铃声越来越难听。这个疯狂的跟踪者曾两次被强制送进精神病院。但之后他又会回来，日复一日地躲在莫里茨班上男孩和女孩的面孔中间，死气沉沉的眼睛，愚蠢而死气沉沉的眼睛，像死神一样怒视着他。

The wild, vitriolic phone calls disturbed Moritz's home night after night, the phone developing an ugly ring. Twice the rabid stalker had been forcibly committed to psychiatric wards. But then he’d be back, day after day nestled amid the human faces of boys and girls in Moritz's class, glaring like death itself with dead eyes, stupid dead eyes.

心脏中了一枪。两枪。另外还有一枪打中了他的肚子和腿。男孩俯身在他身上，挡住了清晨的阳光。“你这个该死的混蛋，接受吧。”他开了四枪，在莫里茨的肉体上撕开了一个洞。“接受吧。”54 岁的莫里茨倒下了，倒在一滩鲜红的血泊中，瘫倒在地，死了。他脸上的震惊和期待的表情消失了，他倒在石头地板上，毫无生气的脸颊受伤了。

There was a gunshot to the heart. Two. Plus a bullet in his guts and one in his leg. The boy hung over him, blocking out the early morning light. “Take that, you damned bastard.” He shot four times, tearing holes in Moritz's flesh. “Take that.” At fifty-four, Moritz fell, crumpled and dead in a pretty pool of bright red blood. The look of shock and anticipation faded from his face as he descended and damaged his lifeless cheek on the stone floor.

随着血液从他破碎的心脏涌入空气，他的大脑窒息，维特根斯坦所有那些未完成的思想都随着神经元的死亡而腐烂。灰质中储存着记忆和想法的神经连接以有序的配置跨越了缝隙，这是精神生命的最后喘息。关于真理和意志的思想被抹去，肉体在雪花石膏上软软地晃动，只剩下腐烂的人类果实。

As the blood poured from his tattered heart into the open air and his brain suffocated, all those incomplete thoughts of Wittgenstein decayed with the dying neurons. Neural connections in the gray matter storing memories and ideas in their ordered configurations fired across the gaps, last gasps of mental life. Thoughts on Truth and Will were erased as flesh sloshed soft and limp against alabaster, no more than rotting human fruit.

在疗养院安静的房间里，有一扇狭窄的窗户在修整过的大草坪上，哥德尔独自一人休息，瘫倒在地，一动不动，心里想着，他去哪儿了？莫里茨在哪儿？

In his quiet room in the sanatorium with the narrow window over the big groomed lawn, Gödel rested alone, slumped and motionless, and wondered, where did he go? Where is Moritz?

说莫里茨生活在过去是什么意思？什么也没有。过去并不存在。过去的概念指的是微不足道、脆弱的记忆，不完整，有缺陷。莫里茨死了。他消失了，只剩下那些在他死后在全球各地迁徙的人们脑海中的碎片。维也纳学派也随他而去，头条新闻谴责莫里茨·石里克是一名犹太同情者，他在维也纳大学的楼梯顶端得到了应有的惩罚，而这位泛日耳曼英雄则正确地杀死了这位犹太哲学家。莫里茨是一名新教徒。世界的事实被封存在脑海中。人们戴着面具。所有的现实都在他们的眼睛后面，那里隐藏着秘密的计划和隐藏的议程。虚假动机和意图的焦油。真相被扭曲了。因为过去并不存在，除了作为许多人较弱的头脑中的一个破烂碎片。莫里茨曾经构想了一个地方和时间，在那里，事情似乎更坚实，更令人信服。库尔特似乎记得自己属于这个地方。莫里茨在哪里？如果他们两人稍有不同，他可能就像库尔特的父亲一样。哪里都没有。他不是真实存在的。

What does it mean to say that Moritz lived in the past? Nothing. The past does not exist. The notion of a past refers to a paltry and brittle memory, incomplete and flawed. Moritz is dead. He is lost but for fragments in the minds of those who have moved around the globe since his death. The Vienna Circle died with him as the headlines condemned Moritz Schlick as a Jew sympathizer who got what he deserved at the top of the stairs in the University of Vienna at the hands of a pan-Germanic hero who rightly killed this Jew philosopher. Moritz was a Protestant. Facts of the world are sealed in minds. People wear a facade. All of reality goes on behind their eyes, and there lie secret plans and hidden agendas. A tar of false motives and intentions. Truth mauled. Because the past does not exist except as a threadbare fragment in the weaker minds of the many. Moritz once framed a place and time in which things seemed more solid, more convincing. A place in which Kurt seems to remember belonging. Where is Moritz, who might— had they both been a little different—have been like a father to Kurt? Nowhere. He is not real.

维也纳，1938 年

VIENNA, 1938

恢复得相当好的哥德尔在桌子上挥舞着一个破信封，信封里折叠着一封信，宣布暂停他的教学权，理由是他之前与莫里茨和几位犹太学者的关系。这种困难激怒了他，他与阿黛尔进行了一次真正的交谈，利用她作为他抱怨的论坛，他详细阐述了法律问题。当他用一个他知道在逻辑上无懈可击的结论来结束他的论点时，他用指关节敲了敲椅子，祈求好运。他希望这个论点能说服当局恢复他在大学的地位。

A much-recovered Gödel waves a torn envelope across the table in which is folded a letter announcing the suspension of his teaching privileges on the basis of his previous association with Moritz and several Jewish scholars. This hardship irritated him into an actual conversation with Adele, using her as a forum for his complaint, which he expounded in legalistic detail. When he sealed his argument with a final conclusion that he knew was logically impeccable, an argument he hoped would persuade the authorities to reinstate his status at the university, he tapped his knuckles on his chair for good luck.

阿黛尔警告说：“迷信会带来厄运。”

“Superstition brings bad luck,” Adele warned.

他想，从统计学上来说，这不太可能，但阿黛尔重新发明了他以前在某个地方听到过的短语并非不可能。“你在哪里听到过这种说法？”

It was statistically unlikely, he thought, but not impossible that Adele reinvented the phrase he has heard somewhere before. “Where did you hear that expression?”

“你在说什么？这是我编的。”

“What are you talking about? I made it up.”

从统计学上讲，这个想法不太可能，但并非不可能。尽管阿黛尔警告过他，他还是再次敲了敲木头，然后重新开始解释，把每一个细节都考虑进去。当阿黛尔终于被他反驳的细节所折服时，他和朋友们一起在咖啡馆里挥舞着撕破的信封，把散落的糖扇成小沙丘。奥托和奥尔加听着这些法律术语，表情有些紧张。

Statistically unlikely, the thought repeated, but not impossible. Despite Adele's warning, he rapped the wood again and then unleashed the entire explanation afresh, incorporating every particular. When Adele was finally worn down with the revisited details of his rebuttal, he joins his friends at the café to wave the torn envelope across the table and fan scattered sugar into small dunes. Otto and Olga listen to the legal jargon with somewhat strained expressions.

当他们通过烟雾和液体品尝到熟悉的味道时，他的朋友们已经开始怀念那个他们知道他们别无选择只能离开的家。奥托生动地向库尔特和奥尔加描述了上个月成千上万的人涌入英雄广场聆听元首演讲的情景——奥尔加只能猜测庞大的人群一定给人留下了怎样的视觉印象。

As they take in familiar tastes through smoke and liquid, his friends already feel nostalgic for a home they know they have no choice but to leave. In vivid bursts, Otto describes for Kurt— and for Olga, who could only guess at the visual impression the massive crowd must have made—hundreds of thousands of people packed onto Heldenplatz last month to hear the Führer.

“他把手放回去接受敬礼的样子看起来有点娘娘腔。”奥托为妻子讲述，仍然能感受到人群的巨大压力。“他看起来很滑稽。他的头晃来晃去，双手张开，脸上的胡须不停地晃动，做出奇怪的动作。”奥托握住她的手，试图淡化他所目睹的纳粹辉煌的海洋。

“He looked a bit effeminate the way he flopped his hand back to accept the salute.” Otto narrates for his wife, still feeling the intense pressure of the crowd. “He looked ridiculous. His head bobbling around and those weird gestures with his hands splayed either side of his jiggling mustachioed face.” Otto takes her hand with this feeble attempt to diminish the overwhelming ocean of Nazi splendor he witnessed.

“奥尔加，他们穿红色。红色与黑色相对。他们偷走了我们的颜色。他们偷走了我们的朋友。每个人都走了或即将离开。维特根斯坦已经离开英国，他的家人已经申请种族分类。根据新法律，他们被视为犹太人。”经过短暂的几乎尴尬的停顿后，他补充道：“我们在这里会非常孤独，我甚至不得不想念维特根斯坦。”

“Olga, they wear red. Red against black. They have stolen our color. They have stolen our friends. Everyone is gone or going. Wittgenstein has left for England and his family has filed for a racial declassification. Under the new laws, they are considered Jews.” And after a momentary almost embarrassed pause he adds, “We are going to be so lonely here that I am even forced to miss Wittgenstein.”

科特很喜欢对这场可怕展览的描述，但似乎并没有受到应有的影响，好像奥托描述的是一部古怪、不可能的电影，而不是他自己家乡的命运。“他们取消了我的教学特权。我不是犹太人，所以从技术上讲，他们没有理由对我采取行动。”

Kurt enjoys the description of the monstrous exhibition but doesn’t seem properly affected, as though Otto is describing an outlandish, impossible movie and not the fate of his own home. “They have revoked my teaching privileges. I am not Jewish, so technically they have no basis for taking action against me.”

奥尔加不耐烦地​​打断了他的话。“科特，这不是重点。”

Olga cuts him off irritably. “Kurt, that is hardly the point.”

奥托也插话道：“他们杀了莫里茨，科特。”他几乎喊道：“我们的莫里茨。”

And Otto cuts in too. “They killed Moritz, Kurt.” He almost shouts, “Our Moritz.”

科特不会让步，他拿出更多文件证据，即使周围出现了新的社会秩序，这些证据也能为他的立场辩护。奥托厌恶地喝着咖啡，奥尔加转过​​身去。他们沉默了好一会儿，科特问道：“奥尔加，莫里茨原谅我了吗？”

Kurt will not relent, offering them further evidence from documents that defend his position even given the new social order pushing up around them. Otto slurps his caffeine in disgust and Olga turns away. They are quiet for a long few minutes when Kurt asks, “Olga, had Moritz forgiven me?”

抽了两口雪茄后，奥尔加终于有耐心回到谈话中，并好奇地问道：“原谅你了？我不太明白你的意思。”

After a full two drags on her cigar, Olga finds the patience to return to the conversation, and the curiosity to ask, “Forgiven you? I’m not sure what you mean.”

“他从来没说过，”但他不知道该怎么结束。他重新调整了一下眼镜。“他从来没提过我的定​​理。”

“He never,” but he doesn’t know how to finish. He realigns his glasses. “He never said a word about my theorems.”

“啊，那个。”奥尔加的眼睛寻找着他，但找不到他。奥尔加对库尔特的印象是一幅速写，定格在二十岁时。奥托多年前就给出了描述，一幅不耐烦的漫画。大大的黑色圆形眼镜。瘦。非常瘦，但脑袋圆圆的。圆头发。他在眼睛下面、镜框后面添加了黑色污迹，这是一个出乎意料但非常值得赞赏的细节。“你的不完全性定理让他很难接受。这对我们所有人来说都很难，对每一位在世的数学家来说都很难。

“Ah, that.” Olga's eyes seek him out but can’t find him. Olga's image of Kurt is a quick drawing frozen at the age of twenty. Otto offered the description years ago, an impatient caricature. Big black round glasses. Thin. Very thin but with a round head. Round hair. He added the dark smudge under the eyes, behind the frames, as an unexpected but much appreciated detail. “Your incompleteness theorem was hard for him to accept. It was hard for all of us, for every mathematician alive.

但莫里茨一直知道，他相信什么并不重要。重要的是真相。不知何故，你会发现它隐藏在我们谁也看不见的地方。我们都意识到数学仍然是完美的——没有悖论，没有矛盾——只是一些无法证明的真理。还不错。我们可以接受这一点。他也可以接受这一点。”

But then Moritz always knew that it did not matter what he believed. What matters is the truth. And somehow you found it hidden where none of us could see. We all came to realize that mathematics is still flawless—no paradoxes, no contradictions—just some truths that cannot be proven. Not so bad. We can live with that. He could live with that.”

“那真是件大事，库尔特。莫里茨的表情……”奥托慷慨的微笑被他哼哼的拳头遮住了。他挥舞着拳头，把拳头和欢笑一起扔了出去。“我们可怜的莫里茨。我非常享受那个晚上。莫里茨需要一点冲突来摆脱维特根斯坦引起的遐想。但我必须承认，我以为你错了。”他用沉重的手揉着胸口，好像那里储存着这些记忆。“我真的以为你错了。”

“That was quite an event, Kurt. Moritz's expression …” Otto's generous smile is obscured behind the fist he snorts into. He tosses the fist away along with the mirth as he swats at the air. “Our poor Moritz. I enjoyed that evening immensely. Moritz needed a little conflict to shake him out of his Wittgenstein-induced reverie. But I have to confess, I thought you were wrong.” He rubs his chest with his heavy hand as if that is where such memories are stored. “I really did think you were wrong.”

“你确实这么做了，对吧？”奥尔加一边回忆，一边扭动着茶杯。“我从一开始就很担心。库尔特，你让我们担心。有一段时间我们确实很难过。如果连算术都不是完整的，那么我们还能从我们的哲学、我们的科学、从那些本来可以拯救我们的东西中得到什么呢？我们曾经紧紧抓住的救星——也许，我现在承认，我们太绝望了——被夺走了。”她靠在奥托身上，把燃烧的雪茄紧紧地抱在胸前。“现在我们又一次希望破灭了。我曾经相信，当我长大后，我会得出某种结论，某种平静的解决方案，然后焦躁就会结束。我会知道一些明确的事情，疑问就会消失。但那永远不会发生。即使是现在，我也接受我们将不得不离开维也纳的事实。我的我的心脏在怦怦跳动。我的肺在运转。我心中充满了疑问，这一刻都在改变，受到自己想法的影响，从未完成，从未解决。

“You did, didn’t you?” Olga twists her cup in its saucer as she remembers. “I myself was worried from the start. Kurt, you worried us. It was hard for us for a time, to be sure. If not even arithmetic is complete, then what could we hope for from our philosophies, from our sciences, from the very things that were to be our salvation? The buoys that we clung to—perhaps, I would admit now, with too much desperation—were taken away.” She leans against Otto and holds her burning cigar close to her chest. “And here we are again with our hopes being crushed. I used to believe that when I was older I would come to some kind of conclusion, some calming resolution, and then the restlessness would end. I would know something definitive and questions would fade. But that will never happen. Even now I accept that we are going to have to leave Vienna. My heart is thumping. My lungs are working. I am full of questions, changing this very second, affected by my own thoughts, never finished, never resolved.

“我们想要构建完整的世界观、完整一致的理论和哲学，以及完美的解决方案，让一切都能找到自己的位置。但我们做不到。当我步行去研究所时，我听到女孩们在公园玩耍，我们的邻居是即将去世的老妇人，我们自己的圈子，我们都珍视一个解决方案、一个令人满意的结局、完整性和统一性，但我们周围却充满了不完整性。

“We wanted to construct complete worldviews, complete and consistent theories and philosophies, perfect solutions where everything could find its place. But we cannot. The girls I hear playing in the park when I walk to the institute, our neighbor the old woman who will die soon, our own circle, we all prize a resolution, a gratifying ending, completeness and unity, but we are surrounded by incompleteness.

“你证明的事情对我们来说很难受。但你是对的，莫里茨也接受了这一点。库尔特，莫里茨钦佩你。他为你感到骄傲。”

“It was hard for us, what you proved. But you were right and Moritz accepted that. Kurt, Moritz admired you. He was proud of you.”

想到这里，科特突然站起来准备离开。他向朋友们道别，虽然已经转过身，但他补充道：“我可能会移民到美国。”

With this thought Kurt stands abruptly to go. He says goodbye to his friends and though already half turned he adds, “I may emigrate to America.”

“你也是吗？”奥托在身后叫道，但科特没有回答。

“You too?” Otto calls after him, but Kurt doesn’t answer.

科特按照计划在离咖啡馆两条街远的公园长椅上遇见了阿黛尔。我看着他们，他们仿佛时间静止了。阿黛尔似乎被公园里的女孩们迷住了。哥德尔一动不动。

Kurt meets Adele, as planned, on a park bench two streets away from the café. They look suspended in time while I watch them. Adele seems distracted by the girls in the park. Gödel is still as stone.

他们离开长凳前往大学，这样 Kurt 就可以提交他对停职的正式且有理有据的回应。他每走一步都会拦住她，以提出自己的论点。Md-complaint，三名年轻男子在他蹒跚地走上台阶时袭击了他。他们穿着棕色衬衫他们的手臂上系着纳粹标志，胸前系着皮带，皮带扣在皮带上。他们看起来像是被套上了哑骡。科特对阿黛尔尖叫。是他的眼镜吗？他的眼镜让他看起来像个犹太人吗？他们踢他、打他，直到他们再也无法忍受阿黛尔无情的殴打，她挥舞着一把危险的雨伞。其中一名棕色衬衫受了重伤。袭击者逃离阿黛尔的打击后，她安抚科特，梳理他的头发，几乎把他带回家修理。在那里，她用清水和药膏给他洗澡，抚摸他柔嫩的背部，哼着歌安抚他破碎的神经。“你看，”她低声说，“迷信会带来厄运。”一个月后，科特永远离开了欧洲，乘船前往美国，环游世界，经过日本，经过辉煌的加利福尼亚，回到绿色而美丽的新泽西州普林斯顿。这次旅行还有另一件非凡的事情。这次他不是独自一人前往美国。这次他带来了自己的辩护人——他的新婚妻子阿黛尔。

They leave the bench for the university so Kurt can submit his formal and finely reasoned response to his suspension. He stops her on each step to the entrance of the university to advance his argument. Md-complaint, three young men attack him on his halting ascent up the steps. They wear brown shirts and ties with swastikas on their arms and leather straps across their chests that buckle onto leather belts. They look harnessed like dumb mules. Kurt screams to Adele. Is it his glasses? Do his glasses make him look Jewish? They kick and hit him until they can no longer bear Adele's merciless beating, wielding as she is a dangerous umbrella. One of the brown shirts is properly injured. After the assailants flee Adele's blows, she calms Kurt, pushes back his hair, and practically carries him home for repairs. There she washes him with fresh water and ointments, strokes his tender back, and hums to him to pacify his shredded nerves. “You see,” she whispers, “superstition brings bad luck.” In a month's time, Kurt leaves Europe forever, traveling to the United States by boat, sailing around the globe, past Japan, past glorious California, to return to green and handsome Princeton, New Jersey. Something else is remarkable about that trip. This time he isn’t traveling to America alone. This time he brings his defense—his new wife, Adele.

纽约市

NEW YORK CITY

我走向公园长椅上的一男一女。他的眼镜、她的手提包、她的雨伞。他们一动不动，犹如石头。他们站在我面前，几步之遥，我经过了绿漆长椅和水泥椅脚。我转过身去看他们，但他们却被时间带走，无法停下脚步，被街道和风带走。

I approach a man and a woman on a park bench. His glasses, her handbag, her umbrella. They’re as still as stone. They’re in front of me and then, within a few strides, I pass the green-painted bench with its concrete feet. I turn to watch them but they are swept along by time, unable to stop, dragged away along with the street and the wind.

我到处都能看到他们，我的两个疯狂宝贝。一个戴着帽子的优雅男人，身边有一个能干、结实的女人。一个孤独的黑发男孩，步履怪异。我在长椅和街道上，在逻辑和代码中寻找。我在寻找被剥离到骨子里的真理。独立于我们而存在的真理，存在于世界上。坚定而无情。我准备接受真理，无论它有多么令人震惊。即使它证明了不完整和人类理性的局限性。即使它证明我们并不自由。

I see them everywhere, my two mad treasures. An elegant man in a hat with an able, stocky woman at his side. A solitary black-haired boy with a peculiar stride. I am looking on benches and streets, in logic and code. I am looking in the form of truth stripped to the bone. Truth that lives independently of us, that exists out there in the world. Hard and unsentimental. I am ready to accept truth no matter how alarming it turns out to be. Even if it proves incompleteness and the limits of human reason. Even if it proves we are not free.

它们就在我们的脑海中，图灵的发光宝石，哥德尔的柏拉图形式。没有社会等级制度。没有种族障碍。与我们的大脑一起被赋予。融入我们的思想结构中 — 没有盲目的信仰，没有永恒诅咒的威胁 — 只有诚实、真理和理性。

They are here in our minds, Turing's luminescent gems, Gödel's Platonic forms. There are no social hierarchies to scale. No racial barriers. Given to us along with our brains. Built into the structure of our thoughts—no bullying into blind faith, no threats of eternal damnation—just honesty, truth, and reason.

我正处于一个未完成的故事之中。我曾经相信，有一天我会得出某种结论，某种平静的解决方案，然后焦躁不安就会结束。但那永远不会发生。即使是现在，我也在向火车走去。我的心怦怦直跳。我的肺在工作。那里有一个男人，一个女人，一张长椅，一副眼镜，一头柔顺的头发，一把雨伞。我们都被困在复杂的遗产之流中——这是人类理性局限性的证明，是我们无限性的证明。这宣告了我们在这个拥挤而孤独的星球上的存在，这宣告了我们很重要，我们是一团活着的灰烬，我们每个人都曾经是某个人，我们为永远无法得到的东西而奋斗，我们可以承认这一点。那就是我们——既有趣又糟糕，同时又伟大。

I am here in the middle of an unfinished story. I used to believe that one day I would come to some kind of conclusion, some calming resolution, and the restlessness would end. But that will never happen. Even now, I am moving toward a train. My heart is thumping. My lungs are working. There is a man, a woman, a bench, the glasses, the smooth hair, an umbrella. We are all caught in the stream of a complicated legacy—a proof of the limits of human reason, a proof of our boundlessness. A declaration that we were down here on this crowded, lonely planet, a declaration that we mattered, we living clumps of ash, that each of us was once somebody, that we strove for what we could never have, that we could admit as much. That was us— funny and lousy and great all at once.

剑桥，1939 年

CAMBRIDGE, 1939

艾伦昨晚在伦敦一家酒店的一间小单人间里住下，房间的窗户可以俯瞰圣詹姆斯广场。此前，艾伦在政府密码学校总部度过了一个下午，这是秘密服务的一个部门。如果他被允许告诉任何人，比如他的母亲或朋友，没有人会比他更惊讶地发现他参与了英国政府的工作。他自己的母亲认为他很聪明，但也许有点无用。他对顺从者（他想象中的秘密服务人员）的宽容并不比他们对他的宽容更大。他甚至曾经加入过反战委员会。但是，当一位在第一次世界大战期间与某些军事组织有过秘密交易的剑桥大学教授无意中听到艾伦在讨论最通用的密码和密码的玩具解决方案时（谁没听到高桌上那刺耳的结巴声？），艾伦引起了英国政府的注意。破译敌人密码的工作信息需要数学家，而不是士兵，所以艾伦被招募了。他确实考虑过拒绝这份机密邀请，但几分钟后，他就知道自己无法抗拒承诺的谜题。如果要打一场战争，艾伦不得不思考自己将扮演什么角色。这份邀请和他的接受，构成了对这个问题一个有点未经深思熟虑的答案的开端。

Alan stayed in London last night in a small single room of a hotel with a window over St. James's Square after an afternoon spent at the headquarters of the Government Code and Cypher School, a division of the secret service. If he were allowed to tell anyone, such as his mother or his friends, no one would be more amazed than he is to find him engaged with His Majesty's government. His own mother considers him brilliant but perhaps somewhat useless. And he feels no more generosity toward conformists, such as he imagines members of the secret service to be, than they feel toward him. He once even joined the Anti-War Council. But when one of the Cambridge dons who had discreet dealings with certain military organizations during the Great War overheard Alan discussing a toy solution for the most general code and cipher (Who didn’t hear that blaring stammer at high table?), Alan was brought to the attention of His Majesty's government. The work of deciphering enemy messages requires mathematicians, not soldiers, so Alan was recruited. He did consider declining the confidential invitation, but within a few minutes he knew he couldn’t resist the promised puzzles. If there is going to be a war, Alan has to wonder what his part will be. The invitation, and his acceptance, forms the beginning of a somewhat unconsidered answer to this question.

他很惊讶在培训课程上看到了一位来自国王学院的古典文学研究员。鉴于参与者需要对课程保密，他觉得自己被这种熟悉感牵连到了一起，仿佛在相互认可的那一刻，他已经泄露了敏感信息。他们一起离开课程时，已经被知识所污染，而当艾伦从草茎上踢出一滴滴淡水时，他们深夜在圣詹姆斯广场漫步，建立了更牢固的保密纽带。他们边走边聊，仿佛害怕停下来，仿佛用他们强劲的步伐推动整个地球绕着地轴旋转，仿佛害怕如果他们停下来会对生态造成影响。他们讨论了核裂变和铀的不稳定同位素。他们讨论了链式反应的可能性以及所涉及的计算。当他们结束讨论时，他们太累了，无法讨论其影响，也太累了，无法入睡。他们回到各自的房间后，每个人都站在自己窗户的昏暗灯光下。如果您此刻站在圣詹姆斯广场抬头仰望这家酒店，您会觉得它们就像是并排矗立着。

He was surprised to see a classics fellow from King's at the training course. Given that the participants were expected to protect the secrecy surrounding the session, he felt implicated by the familiarity, as though he had already leaked sensitive information in the moment of mutual recognition. They left the course together already tainted by knowledge and forged a stronger bond of secrecy by taking wandering laps around St. James's Square late into the night as Alan kicked beads of fresh water off the stalks of grass. They talked while they moved as if afraid to stand still, as if pushing the whole earth around on its axis with their strong strides, as if afraid of the ecological consequences if they were to stop. They discussed nuclear fission and an unstable isotope of uranium. They discussed the potential for a chain reaction and the calculations that were involved. And when they were done they were too tired to discuss the implications and too tired to sleep. Each stood in the dim light of his own window after they retired to their separate rooms. If you had stood in St. James's Square at that moment and looked up at the hotel, it would have seemed as though they were standing nearly side by side.

他们一起坐火车回到剑桥，但沉默。两人没有商量，选择走很长的路回大学。当他们绕过市中心的边缘时，艾伦发现了路德维希·维特根斯坦，他是剑桥大学最近最著名的流亡者，现任哲学教授。维特根斯坦在拐角处停下来和另一个人说话。他们俩都把大衣搭在前臂上。艾伦觉得他看到他们的手在碰触。他听不见他们在说什么，但由于他今天错过了维特根斯坦的课，他不想被人看见。艾伦抓住对方的衣袖，拖着他走过一条狭窄的街道，悄悄地走到他大学宿舍的保护下，在那里他可以用抛硬币开始一个无限的实验。

They rode the train back to Cambridge together but in silence. Without conferring they both opted to walk the long way back to college. As they skirted the edge of the town center, Alan spotted Ludwig Wittgenstein, Cambridge's most noted recent émigré and current professor of philosophy. Wittgenstein was stopped on the corner speaking to another man. They were both holding overcoats draped on their forearms. Alan thought he saw their hands touching. He couldn’t hear what they were saying, but as he had missed Wittgenstein's class today, he didn’t want to be seen. Grabbing the other fellow's coat sleeve, Alan dragged him down a narrow street to weave their way unnoticed to the protection of his college rooms, where he could begin an infinite experiment with the flip of a coin.

维特根斯坦坚持要求艾伦在“数学基础”课上向他的班级宣誓。严格来说，这个誓言是向班级宣誓，而不是向维特根斯坦本人宣誓，尽管感觉上两者没什么区别。艾伦和其他 15 名参加者都宣誓了，但他还是错过了今天上午的讲座。

Wittgenstein insisted that Alan swear an oath to his class on “Foundations of Mathematics.” Strictly speaking the oath was to the class and not to Wittgenstein himself although it felt like much the same thing. Alan made the pledge, as did the other fifteen attendees, but still he missed this morning's lecture.

“由于图灵缺席，今天的讨论将有些插话，”时间一分一秒流逝，艾伦违背誓言的行为也逐渐显露出来，维特根斯坦说道。图灵已成为这位哲学家为消除人们对科学的盲目崇拜而展开的宣传运动的焦点，因此他的数学课已成为他们之间的现场辩论。艾伦不习惯哲学论证方法。艾伦教授的课程恰好有相同的标题，但相比之下，这是一堂关于数学方法的有力课程，是对符号逻辑运算的令人印象深刻的演示，几乎在各个方面都与维特根斯坦的课程相反。尽管如此，艾伦还是他尽力在维特根斯坦的课程中捍卫数学，同时试图坚持更多的哲学论述形式。

“As Turing is absent, today's discussion will be somewhat parenthetical,” Wittgenstein said when the minutes wore on and Alan's violation of his oath became apparent.
